The accusations of Uthman's harsh discipline are rejected by hadith scholars as baseless slander.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative story about a righteous person, do you immediately defend them or do you internally question? This lesson shows that we should not even entertain slander. How often do you allow your mind to dwell on unverified accusations about others, even if you don't speak them? Commit to immediately dismissing such thoughts as false.
?
The scholars said not to build truth on falsehood. In your own life, do you sometimes accept flimsy excuses for your own sins because they are based on half-truths? What would it mean to reject any justification that is built on falsehood?
?
Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi said 'time should not be spent in keeping company with the ignorant.' Who are the 'ignorant' in your life that you waste time arguing with? What would it take to stop that and preserve your time for truth?

Repentance Restores: Defending Abdullah ibn Sa'd
Defends Uthman's appointment of Abdullah ibn Sa'd and rebuts apostasy claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition defends Uthman's appointment of Abdullah ibn Sa'd and rebuts the claim of apostasy through historical evidence and the principle that repentance wipes out past sins.

Core Idea
Sincere repentance erases past sins, even apostasy, restoring a person's standing.

This passage addresses a historical controversy surrounding Abdullah ibn Sa'd, a companion appointed by Uthman. Critics accused him of apostasy, but the author argues that his sincere return to Islam, proven by his subsequent conduct and the participation of other companions in his campaigns, refutes that charge. The lesson explores the historical background and the theological principle that repentance erases past sins.

Guided Path
1
Understand the appointment context
Read about Uthman's appointment of Abdullah ibn Sa'd over war in Egypt, the dispute with Amr ibn al-As, and the reason for Amr's removal. Note the historical details from Ibn Abd al-Barr.
2
Examine the apostasy accusation
Identify the claim that Abdullah ibn Sa'd apostatized after embracing Islam and returned to Mecca. Recognize this as the main objection to his leadership.
3
Learn the rebuttal based on repentance
Focus on the statement 'عاد إلى الإسلام، وظهر إخلاصه والتوبة تجب ما قبلها' (He returned to Islam, his sincerity appeared, and repentance wipes out what came before). Understand how this principle is used to counter the apostasy charge.
This is the core theological argument.
4
See evidence from companions
Review the list of companions and sons of companions who participated in Abdullah ibn Sa'd's Africa campaign. This shows that the early community accepted him and his leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عبد الله بن سعد بن أبي سرح فكان عثمان استعمله على الحرب في مصر، وأبقى عمرو ابن العاص على الخراج ثم وقع خلاف بينهما، فاستقدم عثمان عمرو بن العاص، وصارت إمارتا الخراج والحرب إلى عبد الله بن سعد وذكر ابن عبد البر في كتاب ( الاستيعاب ) سبباً آخر لعزل عمرو بن العاص، هو أن الاسكندرية انتقضت سنة (٢٥هـ) فافتتحها عمرو بن العاص، وعاملهم معاملة الناقضين للعهد، ولم يصح عند عثمان نقضهم، فعزله وولى عبد الله بن سرح فاعتزل عمرو إلى ناحية بفلسطين، وكان يزور المدينة أحياناً . ويدلكم على أن ولاية عبد الله بن سعد لم تقع موقع الإنكار من الصحابة، أن عثمان أمره بفتح أفريقية، فسار إليها في جيش مؤلف من كثير من الصحابة وأبناء الصحابة منهم : عبد الله بن عمر، وعبد الرحمن ابن أبي بكر، وعبد الله بن الزبير، وعبد الله بن جعفر، والحسن والحسين، بن عمرو بن العاص وعبد الله . وقالوا: عبد الله بن سعد هذا كان أسلم ثم ارتد، وعاد إلى مكة، فنقول: عاد إلى الإسلام، وظهر إخلاصه والتوبة تجب ما قبلها، قال أبو بكر بن العربي في ( كتاب العارضة ) : ولّى بن سعد بن أبي سرح لحسن سيرته وحميد طريقته، ولهذا فتح الفتوح في [؟] بحر عثمان عبد الله المغرب وبره [؟] وغزا معه جماعة من الصحابة وأبناء الصحابة، أطاعوه ورضوا عنه.
As for Abdullah ibn Sa'd ibn Abi Sarh, Uthman appointed him over war in Egypt, and kept Amr ibn al-As over the land tax, then a dispute occurred between them, so Uthman recalled Amr ibn al-As, and the two governorships of land tax and war became for Abdullah ibn Sa'd. Ibn Abd al-Barr mentioned in the book al-Isti'ab another reason for removing Amr ibn al-As, which is that Alexandria rebelled in the year 25 AH, so Amr ibn al-As conquered it and treated them as treaty-breakers, but it was not confirmed to Uthman that they had broken the treaty, so he removed him and appointed Abdullah ibn Sarh. Amr then withdrew to an area in Palestine, and he used to visit Medina sometimes. And it indicates to you that the governorship of Abdullah ibn Sa'd did not meet with disapproval from the Companions, that Uthman ordered him to conquer Africa, so he marched to it with an army composed of many Companions and sons of Companions, among them: Abdullah ibn Umar, Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr, Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, Abdullah ibn Ja'far, al-Hasan and al-Husayn, [unclear] bin Amr ibn al-As and Abdullah. And they said: 'This Abdullah ibn Sa'd had embraced Islam then apostatized and returned to Mecca.' We say: He returned to Islam, and his sincerity appeared, and repentance wipes out what came before. Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi said in the book al-Arida: 'He appointed Ibn Sa'd ibn Abi Sarh due to his good conduct and praiseworthy way, and for this he achieved the conquests in [؟] the sea of Uthman Abdullah the Maghrib and its land, and a group of Companions and sons of Companions campaigned with him, obeying him and pleased with him.'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Uthman's decision to appoint Abdullah ibn Sa'd as governor, and a refutation of the accusation that Abdullah ibn Sa'd had apostatized after Islam. The passage begins by explaining the circumstances of the appointment: Uthman initially had Amr ibn al-As over both war and tax, but after a dispute, he separated the roles and gave both to Abdullah ibn Sa'd. Another reason for Amr's removal is given from Ibn Abd al-Barr: the rebellion of Alexandria. The author then argues that the Companions did not object to Abdullah's appointment, as many of them participated in his campaign to Africa. The opposition's claim that Abdullah had apostatized is rebutted by stating that he returned to Islam sincerely, and repentance erases past sins. Ibn al-Arabi is cited to praise Abdullah's character and achievements.

عثمان
أبو بكر بن العربي
الردة
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Past apostasy permanently disqualifies a person from leadership and acceptance in the Muslim community.
Correction: The passage argues that sincere repentance wipes out even apostasy, and a repentant person can be fully rehabilitated, as evidenced by Abdullah ibn Sa'd's leadership and companionship acceptance.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used to defend Abdullah ibn Sa'd against the accusation of apostasy?
A) He never truly apostatized.
B) He repented and returned to Islam sincerely, and repentance wipes out past sins.
C) Uthman forced him to return to Islam.
D) The accusation is false and never happened.

Key Takeaway
Sincere repentance, even from apostasy, can restore a person's standing in Islam, as argued in defense of Abdullah ibn Sa'd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed a sin you committed was too great for God to forgive? After learning that repentance wipes out even apostasy, what is one sin you have been avoiding repenting from because you thought it was beyond forgiveness? Will you repent of it today, trusting in God's mercy?
?
The companions accepted Abdullah ibn Sa'd after his repentance. Is there someone in your life whose past you hold against them, even though they have shown sincere change? How does this lesson challenge your heart to forgive and accept that person?
?
If you truly internalized that sincere repentance erases every past sin, how would your relationship with God change? What specific step will you take this week to turn back to Him with full confidence?

Defending Marwan ibn al-Hakam's Reliability
Defends Marwan ibn al-Hakam against widespread criticism by citing Ibn al-Arabi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some scholars defend Marwan ibn al-Hakam's character against criticism, citing his early service and the testimony of Ibn al-Arabi.

Core Idea
Marwan's character is defended by citing his early service and Ibn al-Arabi's endorsement.

Not all historical figures are judged uniformly. While Marwan ibn al-Hakam is often criticized in some circles, this passage shows that a respected scholar like Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi considered him a just and great figure among the early Muslims. Let's examine this defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to grasp the key facts presented about Marwan.
This is a short passage but carries a strong point.
2
Identify the key claims
Note what the passage says about Marwan: his age at the Prophet's death, his role as secretary for Uthman, and that he narrated hadith from Uthman and Ali. Then note the criticism and the rebuttal.
The rebuttal is from Ibn al-Arabi.
3
Understand the significance of the citation
Ask yourself why Ibn al-Arabi's statement is important. It shows that not all scholars condemn Marwan; some consider him a reliable and just figure.
This connects to the broader study of hadith criticism and sectarian views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Claim of Marwan's Expulsion
Refutes claim of Prophet's exile of Marwan by citing permission for return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rebuttal to the claim that the Prophet expelled Marwan, and how Uthman's return of Marwan was based on prophetic permission and the principle of a ruler's personal knowledge.

Core Idea
Prophetic permission and personal knowledge justify Uthman's return of Marwan.

This lesson examines a specific historical objection raised against the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate: that he returned Marwan to Medina despite the Prophet having exiled him. The Ash'ari rebuttal clarifies that the Prophet himself had granted permission for Marwan's return before his death, and that a ruler may act on personal knowledge in administrative matters. The passage shows careful attention to historical detail and legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Absorb the argument as presented.
Notice the concise style: objection then rebuttal.
2
Identify the objection and rebuttal
The objection is that the Prophet exiled Marwan, yet Uthman returned him. The rebuttal is that the Prophet had already permitted his return. Find these parts in the text.
The objection is implied in the first sentence of the translation.
3
Understand the legal principle
The passage ends with a principle: a ruler may act on personal knowledge outside of litigation. Reflect on why this matters for the argument.
This principle distinguishes administrative from judicial decisions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا رد الحكم إلى المدينة وقد نفاه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم منها إلى الطائف ويدفع هذا بأن النبي قد أذن قبل وفاته لعثمان في رده، ولما أبلغ عثمان هذا الإذن أبا بكر وعمر، قالا له: إن كان معك شهيد رددناه ولما ولي عثمان الخلافة عمل على مقتضى علمه بأن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قد أذن في رده، وللحاكم أن يعمل على ما يعلم في غير القضاء بين الخصوم.
And they said: 'He was returned to Medina, while the Prophet (peace be upon him) had exiled him from it to Ta'if.' This is rebutted by the fact that the Prophet had given permission before his death to Uthman for his return. When Uthman informed Abu Bakr and Umar of this permission, they said to him: 'If you have a witness, we would return him.' When Uthman became caliph, he acted according to his knowledge that the Messenger of God had permitted his return, and a ruler may act according to what he knows outside of litigation between opponents.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its rebuttal. The objection is that the Prophet exiled Marwan to Ta'if, yet Uthman brought him back to Medina. The rebuttal argues that the Prophet had actually permitted Marwan's return before his death, and this was communicated to Abu Bakr and Umar, who requested a witness. As caliph, Uthman acted on his knowledge of that permission. The principle is that a ruler may act on personal knowledge in administrative matters, not just in judicial cases.

Correction: The passage shows that the Prophet himself had permitted Marwan's return before his death, so Uthman's action was based on that permission, not a contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) He ignored the Prophet's exile.
B) The Prophet had given permission for his return before death.
C) He had a new revelation.
D) Abu Bakr and Umar ordered it.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet permitted Marwan's return before his death, and Uthman acted on that knowledge; a ruler may act on personal knowledge in administrative matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
There may be times when you hold a truth that lacks external verification. Do you have the courage to act on it, as Uthman did, or do you let the fear of others' opinions silence you? What would it take for you to stand firm on your knowledge with integrity?

Refuting Claims of Uthman's Harshness
Refutes accusations of Uthman's harshness by citing Ibn al-Arabi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni scholars, particularly hadith scholars like Ibn al-Arabi, responded to accusations of Uthman's harsh treatment of companions.

Core Idea
Hadith scholars firmly deny historical reports of Uthman's mistreatment.

This passage from al-Ibanah addresses serious accusations against Caliph Uthman. After listing claims that Uthman beat companions and withheld stipends, the author presents two lines of defense: the ruler's disciplinary authority and, more emphatically, the denial of these events by hadith scholars. Ibn al-Arabi's categorical rejection sets the tone for how to handle such reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to understand the flow: accusations first, then two responses, then Ibn al-Arabi's decisive statement.
Notice that the passage moves from accusation to rebuttal.
2
Identify the two responses
The passage gives two ways scholars have addressed the accusations. What are they?
One is a general principle, the other is a denial of facts.
3
Focus on Ibn al-Arabi's position
Ibn al-Arabi calls the reports 'falsehood and slander' (زور وإفك). Why does he say this?
He warns against engaging with baseless accusations.
4
Reflect on the lesson for today
Consider how this approach protects the reputation of the Sahaba and guards against fitna.
This is a methodological point about dealing with historical claims.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: كان عمر يضرب بالدرة وهو يضرب بالسوط وضرب عمار بن ياسر وابن مسعود ومنعه عطاءه، وضرب أبا ذر ونفاه إلى الربذة. وسلك بعض أهل العلم في دفع هذا الإنكار طريقة أن للحاكم أن يؤدب بعض الرعايا بمـا يـراه صلاحاً، ولكن حفاظ الحديث أنكروا هذا الذي يحكيه المؤرخون أشد الإنكار، قال أبو بكر بن العربي في ( كتاب العواصم : أما ضربه لعمار وابن مسعود ومنعه لعطاء ابن مسعوده فزور وإفك، وقد اعتذر عن ذلك العلماء بوجوه لا ينبغي أن يُشتغل بها، لأنها مبنية على باطل، ولا يبنى حق على باطل، ولا يذهب الزمان في مماشاة الجهال، فإن ذلك لا آخر له.
And they said: 'Umar used to strike with the whip, while he (Uthman) struck with the lash, he struck Ammar ibn Yasir and Ibn Mas'ud and withheld his stipend, and he struck Abu Dharr and exiled him to al-Rabadha.' Some scholars have taken the approach in rebutting this denial that the ruler may discipline some subjects as he sees fit for reform. But the hadith preservers have denied these things that the historians narrate with the utmost denial. Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi said in the book al-Awasim: 'As for his striking Ammar and Ibn Mas'ud and withholding Ibn Mas'ud's stipend, that is falsehood and slander. The scholars have apologized for it in ways that should not be occupied with, because they are based on falsehood, and right is not built on falsehood. Time should not be spent in going along with the ignorant, for that has no end.'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage addresses accusations that Uthman beat Ammar, Ibn Mas'ud, and Abu Dharr, and withheld stipends. Two responses are given: (1) Some scholars argue the ruler has disciplinary authority. (2) Hadith experts (huffaz) strongly deny that these events even occurred. Ibn al-Arabi categorically calls the stories about beating Ammar and Ibn Mas'ud false and slander. He warns against engaging with such baseless accusations.

Abu Dharr's Voluntary Retreat to al-Rabadha
Narrates Abu Dharr's voluntary exile explained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circumstances of Abu Dharr's retreat to al-Rabadha as a voluntary act of asceticism to avoid fitna, not as a punishment.

Core Idea
Abu Dharr's departure was a voluntary withdrawal to preserve peace.

This lesson examines the account of Abu Dharr's departure to al-Rabadha, a pivotal event often misunderstood in sectarian narratives. The passage from the Ibana presents it as a story of personal piety and discretion, not persecution. We'll read the text, understand its context, and see how it frames the event as a peaceful resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before interpreting.
Notice the key figures and their actions.
2
Identify the sequence of events
Trace the events: Abu Dharr's admonishment → Mu'awiya's complaint → Uthman's summons → gathering → suggestion to withdraw → departure to al-Rabadha.
3
Compare voluntary vs. forced narratives
Ask yourself: Is this presented as a punishment or a respectful retirement? The dialogue and context matter.
This refutes sectarian claims of persecution.
4
Reflect on the portrayal of Uthman and Mu'awiya
Note how Uthman is shown as suggesting a peaceful solution, and Mu'awiya as bringing a complaint. Consider the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قصة أبي ذر فهو أن أبا ذر كان زاهداً، وكان يعظ عمال عثمان في الزهد، ويتلو عليهم قوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ يَكْثِرُونَ الذَّهَبَ وَالْفِضَّةَ وَلَا يُنفِقُونَهَا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ فَبَشِّرْهُم بِعَذَابٍ أَلِيمٍ ) وربما أغلظ لهم القول، فشكاه معاوية إلى عثمان فاستدعاه إلى المدينة فاجتمع عليه الناس فكره ذلك، فقال له عثمان: لو اعتزلت فخرج إلى الربذة زاهداً فاضلاً، ونزل وراءه أجلة فضلاء.
As for the story of Abu Dharr, he was an ascetic, and he used to admonish Uthman's officials about asceticism, reciting to them the words of the Almighty: 'And those who hoard gold and silver and do not spend them in the way of God, give them tidings of a painful torment.' Perhaps he would speak harshly to them. Mu'awiya complained about him to Uthman, so he summoned him to Medina. People gathered around him, and he disliked that. So Uthman said to him: 'If you would withdraw.' So he went out to al-Rabadha as a virtuous ascetic, and eminent virtuous people settled near him.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage recounts the story of Abu Dharr to explain his departure to al-Rabadha. It presents a positive narrative: Abu Dharr was an ascetic who criticized officials for hoarding wealth. Mu'awiya complained, so Uthman called him to Medina. When crowds gathered around Abu Dharr, he disliked it, and Uthman suggested he withdraw. He voluntarily went to al-Rabadha as a respected ascetic, followed by other virtuous people. This reframes the exile as a peaceful, voluntary retirement.

Checkpoint

A) Uthman exiled him as punishment.
B) He chose to withdraw voluntarily after Uthman suggested it.
C) He was fleeing from Mu'awiya.
D) He was forced by the people of Medina.

Key Takeaway
Abu Dharr's departure to al-Rabadha was a voluntary act of asceticism and a means to avoid fitna, not a punishment.

Refutation of Claim that Uthman Climbed the Prophet's Minbar
Denies the report that Uthman climbed the Prophet's minbar above Abu Bakr and Umar's level.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of verifying historical reports and the Sunni position regarding the claim about Uthman climbing the Prophet's minbar.

Core Idea
Historical accusations against the Sahaba must be verified; this report is inauthentic.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a specific accusation against Uthman (RA) that he climbed the Prophet's pulpit to a higher step than Abu Bakr and Umar. The refutation is simple but crucial: the report is not authentic. This lesson focuses on the methodology of rejecting baseless rumors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and literal translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the accusation and the rebuttal.
Note the concise style of the refutation.
2
Identify the accusation and rebuttal
The passage begins with the accusation against Uthman (RA) and then immediately refutes it by declaring the report inauthentic.
3
Understand the citation of Ibn al-Arabi
Imam al-Ash'ari supports the refutation with a quote from Ibn al-Arabi, a respected scholar, who confirms the report is baseless and a munkar rumor.
This shows how Sunni scholarship relies on authoritative references.
4
Reflect on the lesson for historians and Muslims
Consider why it is important to verify historical accounts, especially those concerning the Companions, and how this passage exemplifies that principle.
Think about how rumors can be spread to disturb the hearts of the weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: صعد منبر رسول الله و علا على الدرجة التي كان يقف عليها رسول الله ، وقد انحط عنها أبو بكر وعمر. ويدفع هذا بأن هذه الرواية غير صحيحة، قال أبو بكر بن العربي في ( العواصم : ما سمعت هذا ممن فيه تقية، وإنما هي إشاعة منكر ، ليُروي ويُذكر، ليتغير بها قلب من يتغير.

Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage deals with an accusation that Uthman, in a display of arrogance, climbed to the step of the Prophet's pulpit that even Abu Bakr and Umar had avoided. The rebuttal is straightforward: the report is inauthentic. Ibn al-Arabi says he never heard it from anyone trustworthy; it is a 'munkar' (abominable) rumor spread to disturb people's hearts.

Correction: The report is inauthentic; it was never heard from reliable sources and is classified as a munkar rumor.

Defense of Uthman's Handling of the Hurmuzan Killing
Defends Uthman's decision not to execute Ubaydullah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments used to defend Uthman's decision not to execute Ubaydullah for killing Hurmuzan, including the timing of the event, the complicity of Hurmuzan, and Uthman's payment of blood money.

Core Idea
Uthman's decision was based on precedent, evidence, and a peaceful resolution.

This lesson examines an early Islamic historical incident often used in sectarian debates: the killing of Hurmuzan by Ubaydullah ibn Umar. The passage defends Uthman's handling of the case, arguing that the killing occurred before his caliphate, that Hurmuzan was complicit in Umar's assassination, and that Uthman ultimately resolved the matter by paying blood money from his own wealth. Let's explore the details of this defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusation and rebuttal
Read the opening statement: the accusation that Uthman failed to enforce retaliation. Then immediately read the rebuttal: the incident happened in front of many Companions who remained silent, implying consensus.
2
Examine the historical evidence
The passage cites historians who claimed Hurmuzan was involved in the assassination plot. Understand that this provides a justification for Ubaydullah's action.
3
Note the timing issue
The killing occurred before Uthman assumed authority. This means Uthman was not responsible for the initial action. Consider why this matters for the defense.
4
Understand the resolution
Uthman paid blood money from his own wealth to settle the dispute, ending the conflict. Reflect on how this shows his leadership and sense of responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: لما طعن عمر رضي الله عنه، قتل عبيد الله بن عمر الهرمزان بتهمة أن له يداً في قتل عمر، فلم يقم عثمان على عبيد الله بن عمر حد القصاص. ويدفع بأن هذه الواقعة جرت بمرأى من الصحابة وهم متوافرون، فكيف تقع في أول عهد عثمان ويسكتون عنها؟ وإذا أردنا أن نرجع إلى قول المؤرخين فقد قالوا: إن الهرمزان سعى في قتل عمر وحمل الخنجر، وظهرت تحت ثيابه، وقالوا: أعطى الخنجر لأبي لؤلؤة وحرضه على عمر حتى قتله. وكان قتل عبد الله لـه قبل أن يتولى عثمان، ولعل عثمان كان لا يرى على عبيد الله حقاً، لما ثبت عنده من حال الهرمزان وفعله. عمرو بن العاص لما اختلف الصحابة في الاقتصاص من عبيد الله بن عمر قال لعثمان : إنما كان هذا الحدث ولا سلطان لك، قال عثمان : أنا وليهم وقد جعلتها دية واحتملتها في مالي، وانتهى الخلاف.
And they said: 'When Umar (may God be pleased with him) was stabbed, Ubaydullah ibn Umar killed Hurmuzan on suspicion that he had a hand in killing Umar, and Uthman did not enforce the penalty of retaliation on Ubaydullah ibn Umar.' This is rebutted by the fact that this incident took place in the sight of the Companions while they were abundant; how could it occur at the beginning of Uthman's reign and they remain silent about it? And if we wish to refer to the statement of historians, they said: 'Hurmuzan had sought to kill Umar, carried the dagger, and it appeared under his clothes, and they said: He gave the dagger to Abu Lu'lu'a and incited him against Umar until he killed him.' And Ubaydullah's killing of him was before Uthman assumed authority. Perhaps Uthman did not see any right (of retaliation) against Ubaydullah, because the case of Hurmuzan and his action was established to him. When the Companions disagreed about retaliating against Ubaydullah, Amr ibn al-As said to Uthman: 'This incident happened when you had no authority.' Uthman said: 'I am their guardian, and I have made it a blood money and borne it from my wealth.' And the disagreement ended.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage addresses the accusation that Uthman failed to apply the death penalty to Ubaydullah for killing Hurmuzan. The defense makes several points: (1) The incident happened before Uthman's caliphate (it was during the transition after Umar's death). (2) The Companions were present and did not object, so the claim of injustice is suspect. (3) Historians actually indicate Hurmuzan was complicit in Umar's assassination, so killing him may have been justified. (4) Uthman resolved the dispute by paying blood money from his own wealth, which calmed the situation.

Key Terms
عمر
عبيد الله بن عمر
الهرمزان
القصاص
الدية
عثمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman deliberately ignored justice and allowed a murderer to go free.
Correction: The passage shows the killing occurred under different circumstances, Hurmuzan was implicated, and Uthman paid blood money to resolve the matter peacefully.

Checkpoint
How did Uthman ultimately resolve the case of Ubaydullah killing Hurmuzan?
A) He executed Ubaydullah.
B) He imprisoned Ubaydullah.
C) He paid blood money (دية) from his own wealth.
D) He exiled both parties.

Key Takeaway
Uthman defended his decision not to execute Ubaydullah by pointing to the Companions' silence, Hurmuzan's guilt, the timing of the event, and by paying blood money from his own purse to restore harmony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When others silently witness an action you take, do you assume their silence means approval, as Uthman did with the Companions? How can you ensure you are not misinterpreting silence as consent, especially in matters of justice?
?
Uthman bore a financial burden to end a dispute, even though the initial act happened before his rule. Is there a conflict in your life—past or present—where you need to sacrifice your own resources (time, money, pride) to restore harmony, regardless of who was at fault?
?
The passage uses historical reports about Hurmuzan's guilt to justify his killing. How carefully do you investigate accusations before forming judgments about others? Are there people you have treated harshly based on hearsay, and how can you seek the truth as Uthman did?

Defending Uthman's Compilation of the Quran
Defends Uthman's unification of Quranic readings and burning of variant copies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind Uthman's standardization of the Quranic text and the rebuttal against accusations of innovation.

Core Idea
Uthman's preservation was divine, not innovation.

This lesson examines a common accusation against Uthman regarding his compilation of the Quran. The passage defends his actions by linking them to God's promise of preservation and explains the purpose of burning variant codices.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage (الدفاع عن جمع عثمان للمصحف) aloud, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: accusation, then rebuttal with Quranic proof and explanation.
Focus on the logical flow from accusation to response.
2
Identify the accusation and the divine promise
Identify the accusation: 'He innovated in collecting the codex and burning the codices.' Then find the divine promise: Quran 15:9. How does the author connect God's promise to Uthman's action?
The key is that preservation is by God, but through human means.
3
Analyze the purpose of burning codices
Read the explanation by Ibn Hazm: Uthman only burned codices that contained possible errors or heretical insertions. Reflect on how this defends Uthman's action as protective rather than destructive.
This nuance is crucial for understanding the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: ابتدع في جمع المصحف وحرق المصاحف. ويدفع هذا الطعن بأن الله تعالى وعد بحفظ القرآن فقال تعالى : ( إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِّكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفَظُونَ ) فكان هذا الحفظ على يد عثمان، فإن عثمان - كما يقول ابن حزم - جمع القرآن في مصحف تحفظ بـه القراءات المسموعة من النبي ، وإنما حرق المصاحف التي تشتمل على شيء يتوهمه واهم، أو يدسه زنديق.
And they said: 'He innovated in collecting the codex (mushaf) and burning the codices.' This accusation is rebutted by the fact that God Almighty promised to preserve the Quran, saying: 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We are its Preservers.' So this preservation was at the hands of Uthman. For Uthman – as Ibn Hazm says – collected the Quran in a codex that preserves the recitations heard from the Prophet, and he only burned the codices that contained something that a person might imagine (incorrectly) or that a heretic might insert.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defends Uthman's standardization of the Quranic text and burning of other manuscripts. The accusers call it an innovation. The rebuttal is that God promised to preserve the Quran, and He used Uthman as the instrument. Citing Ibn Hazm, the passage explains that Uthman's codex preserved the authentic recitations, and he burned only those codices that could cause confusion or contain interpolations. Thus, the act was protective, not innovative.

Correction: The passage argues it was an act of preservation, eliminating only those copies that contained errors or potential heretical additions.

Checkpoint

A) To destroy all versions but his own.
B) Because they contained incorrect readings or possible interpolations.
C) He was ordered by the companions to burn them.
D) The copies were worn out.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's compilation and burning of Quranic codices were not innovations but divinely guided measures to preserve the authentic text and prevent corruption.

?

?

?
If you were in Uthman's position, would you have had the courage to burn manuscripts for the sake of unity, even knowing you'd be accused of destroying divine words? What does your hesitation (or lack thereof) reveal about your trust in God's promise to preserve?

Uthman's Full Prayer at Mina: Ijtihad in the Face of Misunderstanding
Justifies Uthman's full prayer at Mina as ijtihad to prevent misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Uthman's performance of the full four-rak'at prayer at Mina was a deliberate ijtihad aimed at preventing a greater harm, not a violation of the sunna.

Core Idea
Uthman's full prayer at Mina was ijtihad to teach the obligation, not to abandon the sunna.

This passage addresses a historical criticism against Uthman ibn Affan for praying four rak'at at Mina instead of the usual shortening to two. The defense explains that Uthman acted out of concern that Bedouins might misunderstand the sunna as reducing the obligatory prayer. The passage thus illustrates how a companion's ijtihad may prioritize preventing a greater evil over following a sunna.

Guided Path
1
Read the criticism and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to see the objection that Uthman contravened the sunna, and the answer provided.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then defense.
2
Understand Uthman's reasoning
Notice how Uthman's own sermon and the incident with the Bedouin show his intention: he feared people would adopt two rak'at as the norm.
3
Reflect on the concept of ijtihad
Consider Ibn al-Arabi's explanation that this was ijtihad – a ruling based on preventing a means to sin (sadd al-dhara'i').
This connects to broader principles of Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: خالف سنة القصر في الصلاة فصلى بالناس في منى أربع ركعات، وكان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبــو بكر وعمر يصلونها ركعتين. وجواب هذا أن تلك السنة كثر فيها الأعراب، فخشي أن يسبق إلى أذهانهم أن الصلاة اثنان، فقصد إلى تعليمهم أنها أربع. وروى البيهقي أن عثمان نفسه خطب في منى فقال: إن القصر سنة رسول الله وصاحبيه ولكن حدث طَغَام فخفت أن يستنوا. ولعل الذي نبهه إلى هذا ما روي أن أعرابياً ناداه في منى يا أمير المؤمنين ما زلت أصليها منذ رأيتك عام أول ركعتين. قال ابن العربي في ( العواصم : وأما ترك عثمان للقصر فاجتهاد، إذ سمع أن الناس افتتنوا بالقصر وفعلوه في منازلهم، فرأى أن السنة ربما أدت إلى إسقاط الفرض، فتركها خوف الذريعة.
And they said: 'He contravened the sunna of shortening (qasr) in prayer, for he prayed with the people at Mina four rak'at, while the Prophet (peace be upon him) and Abu Bakr and Umar used to pray two rak'at.' The answer to this is that at that time, many Bedouins were present, and he feared that it might occur to their minds that the prayer is two (rak'at), so he intended to teach them that it is four. Al-Bayhaqi narrated that Uthman himself gave a sermon at Mina saying: 'Shortening is the sunna of the Messenger of God and his two companions, but riff-raff have appeared, and I feared that they would adopt it (as a norm).' Perhaps what alerted him to this is what was narrated that a Bedouin called out to him at Mina: 'O Commander of the Faithful, ever since I saw you last year, I have been praying two rak'at.' Ibn al-Arabi said in al-Awasim: 'As for Uthman's abandonment of shortening, it was ijtihad, because he heard that people had become infatuated with shortening and were doing it in their homes, so he saw that the sunna might lead to the abandonment of the obligatory (prayer), so he left it for fear of the means (to sin).'
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage addresses the criticism that Uthman prayed four rak'at at Mina instead of the usual shortening (qasr) to two. The defense is that he did this intentionally to teach ignorant Bedouins that the full prayer is four rak'at, lest they think two is always sufficient. Uthman himself explained that he feared people would adopt shortening as a permanent practice, leading to neglect of the obligatory four. Ibn al-Arabi calls it ijtihad: Uthman saw that people were becoming careless, so he prioritized teaching the full obligation over following the sunna of shortening, as a precautionary measure.

Key Terms
القصر
منى
الأعراب
الاجتهاد
الذريعة
عثمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman's action was a violation of the established sunna of shortening prayer.
Correction: Uthman's action was a deliberate ijtihad to prevent the greater harm of people neglecting the obligatory four rak'at.

Checkpoint
What was Uthman's stated reason for praying four rak'at at Mina instead of shortening?
A) He forgot the sunna.
B) He wanted to teach the Bedouins that the prayer is four rak'at.
C) He was forced by the people.
D) He had a new revelation.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's full prayer at Mina demonstrates that a companion's ijtihad may sometimes override a sunna to prevent a greater evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you knowingly chosen to do something that might be criticized by others because you saw a greater good? Did you have the courage to explain your reasoning, like Uthman did in his sermon?
?
Is there a religious practice you hold onto out of habit, even if it might cause others to misunderstand the deeper obligation? What would it take for you to adjust for the sake of clarity, even at the cost of being seen as inconsistent?
?
Uthman feared that people would abandon an obligation because of a sunna. What obligation in your own life are you neglecting because you are too focused on a recommended practice? How can you rebalance your priorities today?

The Imamate of the Four Rightly Guided Caliphs
Ash'ari establishes the imamate of Ali and the four caliphs with consensus and a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari defends the imamate of Ali and the four caliphs using consensus (ijma') and a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The caliphate of the four rightly guided caliphs is validated by consensus and prophecy.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari provides both rational and textual evidence for the legitimacy of the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali. He particularly addresses the position of Ali, showing that his imamate was established by the agreement of the community and that his earlier restraint was virtuous. A hadith from the Prophet confirms that the caliphate would last thirty years, which Safinah found exactly fulfilled by the reigns of these four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two main sections: a rational argument for Ali's imamate and a hadith proof for all four.
Pay attention to the terms 'ahl al-ḥall wa l-ʿaqd' and 'ahl al-shūrā'.
2
Identify the argument for Ali's imamate
Ash'ari states that Ali's imamate is established by a contract (ʿaqd) from the people of loose and binding, and because no one else claimed it. Also, his earlier restraint was proper.
This shows that Ali's caliphate was legitimate and he was the rightful successor after Uthman.
3
Analyze the hadith of thirty years
The hadith from Safinah reports the Prophet saying the caliphate will be thirty years. Safinah counts the reigns of the four caliphs and finds exactly thirty years.
This hadith is used as textual proof for the legitimacy of all four caliphs.
4
Connect the hadith to the four imams
Ash'ari concludes that the hadith indicates the imamate of the four imams (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) collectively. The thirty years cover their combined rule.
This refutes any claim that Ali's caliphate was not part of the prophetic caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتثبت إمامة علي رضي الله عنه بعد عثمان رضي الله عنه بعقد من عقدها له من الصحابة من أهل الحل والعقد، ولأنه لم يدع (۱) أحد من أهل الشورى غيره في وقته، وقد أجمع على فضله وعدله، وأن امتناعه عن دعوى الأمر لنفسه في وقت الخلفاء قبله كان حقاً لعلمه أن ذلك ليس بوقت قيامه ثم لما صار الأمر إليه أظهر وأعلن ولم يقصر حتى مضى على السداد والرشاد كما مضى من قبله من الخلفاء وأئمة العدل على السداد والرشاد متبعين لكتاب ربهم وسنة نبيهم . هؤلاء هم الأئمة الأربعة المجمع على عدلهم وفضلهم رضي الله عنهم أجمعين (۲) وقد روى شريح بن النعمان قال حدثنا حشرج بن نباتة، عن سعيد بن جمهان، قال: حدثني سَفِينَةُ قَالَ: قَالَ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: ( الْخِلافَةُ فِي أُمَّتِي ثَلاثُونَ سَنَةً، ثُمَّ مُلْكُ بَعْدَ ذلك ) ثُمَّ قَالَ لي سَفِينَةُ : أَمْسِكَ خِلافَةَ أَبي وخلافة عُمَرَ وَخِلافَةَ عُثْمَانَ، ثُمَّ قَالَ: أَمْسِكَ خِلافَةَ عَلِيٍّ بن أبِي طَالِبٍ، قَالَ: فَوَجَدْتُهَا ثَلاثِينَ سَنَة (۳). فدل ذلك على إمامة الأئمة الأربعة رضي الله عنهم أجمعين .
And the imamate of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, after Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, is established by a contract made for him by the companions among the people of loose and binding (ahl al-ḥall wa l-ʿaqd), and because no one among the people of the consultative council (ahl al-shūrā) claimed it other than him in his time, and there was consensus on his virtue and justice, and that his refraining from claiming the authority for himself during the time of the caliphs before him was a right due to his knowledge that that was not the time for his rise. Then when the matter came to him, he manifested and announced it and did not fall short until he passed away on rectitude and right guidance, as those before him from the caliphs and the imams of justice passed away on rectitude and right guidance, following the Book of their Lord and the Sunnah of their Prophet. These are the four imams upon whose justice and virtue there is consensus, may Allah be pleased with them all. And Shurayḥ ibn al-Nuʿmān narrated, saying: Ḥashraj ibn Nubātah narrated to us from Saʿīd ibn Jamhān, who said: Safīnah narrated to me, saying: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'The caliphate in my community will be thirty years, then there will be kingship after that.' Then Safīnah said to me: 'Count the caliphate of Abu Bakr, the caliphate of Umar, and the caliphate of Uthman, then he said: Count the caliphate of Ali ibn Abi Talib.' He said: So I found it to be thirty years. So that indicates the imamate of the four imams, may Allah be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the imamate of the four rightly guided caliphs, focusing especially on Ali. The author begins by stating that Ali's imamate is established by a contract from the companions (ahl al-ḥall wa l-ʿaqd) and because no one else among the consultative council claimed it at that time. There was consensus on his virtue and justice, and his earlier refraining was appropriate. After becoming caliph, he acted righteously like his predecessors. The author then identifies these four as the imams upon whose justice there is consensus. He then supports this with a hadith from Safīnah, who counted the reigns of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali to be thirty years, fulfilling the Prophet's prophecy. This hadith thus demonstrates the legitimacy of the imamate of the four caliphs.

Key Terms
إمامة
أهل الحل والعقد
أهل الشورى
الخلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith of thirty years excludes Ali because his reign is sometimes considered part of the subsequent kingship.
Correction: Safinah explicitly counts Ali's reign as part of the thirty years, proving that Ali's caliphate is included in the prophetic caliphate.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, how many years did the caliphate last?
A) 20 years
B) 25 years
C) 30 years
D) 40 years

Key Takeaway
The four rightly guided caliphs, including Ali, are all legitimate imams whose caliphate fulfills the prophetic prophecy of thirty years.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you, like Ali, refrained from pursuing a rightful position because you knew it was not yet Allah's timing? Are you willing to trust His plan even when it delays your own ambitions?
?
The passage says Ali followed 'the Book of their Lord and the Sunnah of their Prophet' in his leadership. Is your own leadership—at home, work, or in your community—similarly grounded in revelation, or do you rely on your own understanding?
?
The hadith shows the caliphate was only thirty years. How does this prophecy affect the way you view the political history of Islam? Do you see the shift to kingship as a decline, and what does that demand from you in terms of loyalty to Muslim rulers today?

Conflicts Among Companions Based on Ijtihad
Ash'ari explains that conflicts among Companions were based on ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the conflicts among the Companions were based on legitimate ijtihad and that both sides were upon truth.

Core Idea
Even conflicts among the best of people can arise from valid interpretation.

This lesson addresses a sensitive topic: the historical conflicts among the Prophet's Companions. Imam Ash'ari provides a principled explanation that avoids condemning either side. The key is the concept of ijtihad—independent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning. Let the author's reasoning come through.
Notice how Ash'ari uses the phrase 'taʼwīl and ijtihad' as the basis for the conflicts.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the words 'taʼwīl' (interpretation) and 'ijtihad' (independent reasoning). Understand that these are technical terms in Islamic jurisprudence, not excuses for wrongdoing.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ash'ari argues that all involved were upon truth because the Prophet testified for them of paradise. Consider why this is an important theological point for Sunni Islam.
This is the key takeaway: the conflicts were not sinful, but based on valid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما جرى بين علي والزبير وعائشة رضي الله عنهم أجمعين، فإنما كان على تأويل واجتهاد وعلي الإمام، وكلهم من أهل الاجتهاد وقد شهد لهم النبي بالجنة والشهادة ، فدل على أنهم كلهم كانوا على حق في اجتهادهم (۱) وكذلك ما جرى بين علي ومعاوية رضي الله عنهما كان على تأويل واجتهاد .
As for what occurred between Ali, al-Zubayr, and Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them all, it was only based on interpretation (taʼwīl) and ijtihad, and Ali was the imam, and all of them were people of ijtihad, and the Prophet had testified for them of paradise and martyrdom, so that indicates that all of them were upon truth in their ijtihad. Similarly, what occurred between Ali and Muawiya, may Allah be pleased with both of them, was based on interpretation and ijtihad.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage addresses historical conflicts among the companions, specifically the Battle of the Camel (Ali, Zubayr, Aisha) and the conflict between Ali and Muawiya. The author states that these events were based on taʼwīl (interpretation) and ijtihad (independent reasoning). Ali was the legitimate imam at that time, and all parties were qualified for ijtihad. Since the Prophet had promised them paradise and martyrdom, all of them were correct in their ijtihad, meaning they were not sinful. Similarly, the conflict with Muawiya was also based on ijtihad. This is a key Sunni position that avoids condemning either side.

Key Terms
تأويل
اجتهاد
إمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One party was wrong and sinful in the conflicts among the Companions.
Correction: Both sides were acting on valid ijtihad and were upon truth, as affirmed by the Prophet's testimony of paradise.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the conflicts among the companions?
A) They were based on sin
B) They were based on ijtihad
C) They were based on ignorance
D) They were based on personal enmity

Key Takeaway
Conflicts among the Companions were based on ijtihad, and all involved were upon truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see disagreements among Muslims today, do you tend to assume one side is entirely wrong and sinful? How does knowing that the Companions were all correct in their ijtihad despite sharp conflict challenge your default reaction?
?
Have you ever judged someone harshly based on a conflict that you did not fully understand? This lesson teaches that even the best of humanity were upon truth in their disagreements. How can you apply this humility to your own disagreements with others, especially in matters of interpretation?
?
If you were in the position of Ali or Aisha, would you have the same humility to accept that your opponent might also be upon truth? What would it take for you to assume good faith in someone you sharply disagree with?

The Trustworthiness of All Companions
Ash'ari asserts the trustworthiness of all companions and the duty to honor them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that all companions of the Prophet are trustworthy and must be honored and loved.

Core Idea
All companions are trustworthy; loving them is mandatory.

This passage concludes Imam al-Ash'ari's 'Al-Ibanah' with a clear statement on the status of the Prophet's companions. It affirms that every companion is a reliable guide in religion and that Allah and His Messenger have praised them all. The author then outlines the believer's duty towards them: honor, respect, loyalty, and dissociation from those who criticize any of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text slowly, then check the English translation. Notice the author's emphatic opening 'Wa kullu as-sahabati a'immah ma'munun'.
The passage is the final statement of the entire book.
2
Identify the key claims
The passage makes three main claims: all companions are imams and trustworthy, Allah and His Messenger have praised them all, and we are commanded to honor them and disavow those who disparage them.
These claims form the core of the Sunni position on companions.
3
Reflect on the commanded actions
Note the four actions mentioned: tawqir (honor), ta'zim (respect), muwalah (loyalty), and tabarri (disassociation) from anyone who diminishes a companion. Consider how these apply in practice.
4
Consider the context as a conclusion
This passage ends the book. Think about why the author chose to conclude with this topic—it is a foundational creedal point that sums up the proper attitude towards the Prophet's companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل الصحابة أئمة مأمونون غير مُتهمين في الدين، وقد أثنى الله ورسوله على جميعهم، وتعبدنا بتوقيرهم وتعظيمهم وموالاتهم، والتبري من كل من ينقص أحداً منهم، رضي الله عنهم أجمعين. وقد قلنا في الأبرار قولاً وخبراً، والحمد لله أولاً وآخراً . تم كتاب الإبانة عن أصول الديانة ) بعون الله الملك الوهاب، وحسن توفيقه والصلاة والسلام على رسوله محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين
And all the companions are imams who are trustworthy, not accused in religion. Allah and His Messenger have praised all of them, and we are commanded to honor them, respect them, and show loyalty to them, and to disassociate from anyone who disparages any of them. May Allah be pleased with them all. We have spoken about the righteous (al-abrār) a word and a report. All praise is to Allah, first and last. Completed is the book 'Al-Ibanah 'an Usul al-Diyanah' with the help of Allah, the King, the Bestower, and His good guidance. And prayers and peace be upon His Messenger Muhammad and his family and all his companions.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement on the status of the companions. The author declares that all companions are imams and trustworthy (maʾmūnūn), not accused in matters of religion. This is supported by the praise of Allah and His messenger for all of them. The author then states the obligation (taʿabbada) to treat them with respect and reverence, to show loyalty (muwālāt) to them, and to disassociate (tabarrī) from anyone who diminishes any of them. The author concludes with praise to Allah and the completion of the book. This affirms the Sunni doctrine of the uprightness of all companions.

?

The Correct Status of All Companions
Author concludes by affirming the correctness of all Companions and the duty to honor them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari stance on the Companions: that all were correct in their ijtihad, that the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah was based on interpretation, and that we must honor all Companions and dissociate from those who disparage them.

Core Idea
All Companions are trustworthy; we honor them and reject their disparagers.

Imam al-Ash'ari concludes his book with a clear and definitive statement about the Companions of the Prophet. In this passage, he affirms their collective correctness, addresses the historical conflict between Ali and Muawiyah, and sets forth the duties of Muslims toward the Sahaba. This final lesson ties together the Ash'ari commitment to unity and respect for the entire generation of the Prophet's companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the three main parts: the general affirmation, the specific conflict, and the duties toward the Companions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the argument
Identify how the author uses the Prophet's testimony to support the correctness of all Companions in ijtihad. Then note how he applies the same principle to the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah.
This refutes any view that one party was in error.
3
Apply the lesson
Reflect on the practical obligations: honor, veneration, loyalty to all Companions, and dissociation from anyone who disparages them. Consider how this shapes your attitude toward the Sahaba.
This is a binding religious duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاجتهاد وقد شهد لهم النبي بالجنة والشهادة ، فدل على أنهم كلهم كانوا على حق في اجتهادهم . وكذلك ما جرى بين علي ومعاوية رضي الله عنهما كان على تأويل واجتهاد . وكل الصحابة أئمة مأمونون غير مُتهمين في الدين، وقد أثنى الله ورسوله على جميعهم، وتعبدنا بتوقيرهم وتعظيمهم وموالاتهم، والتبري من كل من ينقص أحداً منهم، رضي الله عنهم أجمعين. وقد قلنا في الأبرار قولاً وخبراً، والحمد لله أولاً وآخراً . تم كتاب الإبانة عن أصول الديانة ) بعون الله الملك الوهاب، وحسن توفيقه والصلاة والسلام على رسوله محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين (۱) انظر ( شرح الطحاوية ، ص (٥٤٥-٥٥٢) .
Ijtihad – and the Prophet testified for them with Paradise and testimony, so it indicates that all of them were correct in their ijtihad. And likewise what occurred between Ali and Muawiyah, may Allah be pleased with them, was based on interpretation and ijtihad. And all the Companions are imams who are trustworthy, not accused in religion; Allah and His Messenger praised all of them, and He bound us to honor them, venerate them, show loyalty to them, and dissociate from anyone who disparages any one of them. May Allah be pleased with them all. And we have said regarding the righteous a saying and a report. Praise be to Allah, first and last. The book Al-Ibanah fi Usul al-Diyanah is completed with the help of Allah, the King, the Bestower, and His good success. Blessings and peace be upon His Messenger Muhammad, his family, and his Companions all. (1) See (Sharh al-Tahawiyyah, p. 545-552).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is the final section of Al-Ibanah, where the author concludes with a concise statement about the Companions of the Prophet. It is a three-part declaration. First, the author affirms that all Companions who engaged in ijtihad (independent legal reasoning) were correct in their conclusions, as the Prophet himself testified that they would enter Paradise. This is a direct refutation of any view that some Companions were in error. Second, he specifically addresses the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah, stating that it was a matter of legitimate interpretation and ijtihad, implying that neither party is to be blamed. Third, he lays out the proper attitude toward all Companions: they are trustworthy imams; Allah and His Messenger praised them; and Muslims are obligated to honor, venerate, and show loyalty to them, while dissociating from anyone who disparages any of them. The passage then closes with a prayer of praise to Allah and the colophon of the book. The footnote references a discussion in Sharh al-Tahawiyyah for further reading. This final lesson encapsulates the Ash'ari stance on the Sahaba, emphasizing unity and respect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position condemns one party in the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah.
Correction: The author clarifies that both were engaging in legitimate ijtihad, and neither is to be blamed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct attitude towards someone who speaks ill of any Companion?
A) To ignore them
B) To debate them politely
C) To dissociate from them
D) To consider them as having a valid opinion

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school maintains that all Companions were correct in their ijtihad, and Muslims must honor them and dissociate from those who disparage them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says we must dissociate from anyone who disparages any Companion. Is there a single Companion you have ever criticized, even in your heart? What will you do today to replace that thought with love and respect?
?
Both Ali and Muawiyah were correct in their ijtihad despite their conflict. Can you think of a person you have condemned over a disagreement? How can you apply this lesson to stop judging others' intentions and trust that they too may be acting on sincere ijtihad?
?
The Prophet's testimony guarantees the correctness of all Companions. Do you truly believe that every single one of them is better than any Muslim after them? If your actions show otherwise, what is one practical step you will take to raise their status in your heart and tongue?
